Title: Inference for Fixed Effects Estimators when Panels are Unbalanced

URL Source: https://arxiv.org/pdf/2607.10246v1

Published Time: Tue, 14 Jul 2026 00:40:09 GMT

Number of Pages: 39

Markdown Content:
# Inference for Fixed Effects Estimators when Panels are Unbalanced вҲ—

Daniel Czarnowske вҖ  Amrei Stammann вҖЎ

July 14, 2026 We derive the asymptotic properties of two-way fixed effects M-estimators with missing observations in an asymptotic framework in which the numbers of cross-sectional units and time periods grow jointly. We allow the selection process to be deterministic (conditional on the unobserved effects and initial conditions), stochastic, or mixed, and we impose only a conditional mean restriction. The uncorrected estimators are asymptotically normal but not centered at zero, suffering from incidental parameter and feedback biases. Feedback bias can be induced by predetermined regressors in the outcome equation and by a predetermined selection process. We propose debiased estimators that handle both sources without requiring knowledge of which regressors or selection components are predetermined. 

JEL Classification: C13, C23 

Keywords: panel data, unbalanced panel data, dynamic model, two-way fixed effects, incidental parameter problem, asymptotic bias correction.

> вҖЎ

UniversitГӨt Bayreuth, UniversitГӨtsstr. 30, 95447 Bayreuth, Germany; e-mail: 

amrei.stammann@uni-bayreuth.de arXiv:2607.10246v1 [econ.EM] 11 Jul 2026 1 Introduction 

Although unbalanced panel data are common in empirical work, the theoretical econometrics literature focuses mostly on balanced panels. Missing observations may arise because units enter and exit the sample depending on their unobserved heterogeneity, or because nonresponse depends on past outcomes. Both mechanisms matter for inference. We provide the first complete theoretical analysis of two-way fixed effects M-estimators in a large-рқ‘Ғ, рқ‘Ү asymptotic framework that accommodates missing observations due to sample selection and attrition. Throughout the paper, we refer to the process that generates the missing observations as the selection process. Whereas the literature typically considers the selection process to be either deterministic (conditional on the unobserved effects and initial conditions) or stochastic (see, e.g., Chapter 17 of Hansen (2022)), we introduce a mixed process that encompasses both pure cases. For the stochastic component, we build on Chapter 19 of Wooldridge (2010) and impose a general conditional mean restriction rather than a full conditional independence assumption. We derive the asymptotic distribution of the fixed effects estimators and characterize their bias and variance. In contrast to balanced panels, both bias and variance depend on the selection process. The uncorrected estimators are asymptotically normal but not centered at zero. We derive debiased estimators whose asymptotic distributions are centered at zero. Our contributions also have important implications for applied work. Predetermined regressors introduce feedback bias that must be corrected for valid inference. This holds not only for predetermined regressors in the outcome equation, such as a lagged outcome, but also for those in the selection process. Feedback bias can therefore arise even when the outcome equation depends solely on strictly exogenous regressors. A key advantage of our bias correction is that it requires no knowledge of which regressors are predetermined, whether in the outcome equation or in the selection process. Furthermore, the selection process introduces an additional layer of heterogeneity, leading to two consequences. First, when the design depends on unobserved effects, the heterogeneity carries over to the subsamples on which jackknife corrections are based, invalidating them, as our simulations confirm. Our analytical correction does not require homogeneity. Second, the additional heterogeneity can generate heteroskedasticity, which conventional heteroskedasticity-robust standard errors can address. 

Related literature. Our paper is related to the large-рқ‘Ғ, рқ‘Ү literature on fixed effects estimators. This strand uses bias correction methods to address the inference problems caused by incidental parameters, first identified by Neyman and Scott (1948) and Nickell (1981). Prominent debiasing methods include analytical and jackknife corrections (see, e.g., Hahn and Newey (2004)), split-panel jackknife corrections (see, e.g., Dhaene and Jochmans (2015)), and bootstrap corrections (see, e.g., Kim and Sun (2016)). Most contributions focus on one-way fixed effects estimators. For panel models with two-way unobserved effects, as we consider here, the literature is sparser. FernГЎndez-Val and Weidner (2016) develop the first asymptotic theory of fixed effects estimators 1for such models and derive analytical and split-panel jackknife bias corrections. Other work has focused on more specialized settings, such as logit or network models, or strictly exogenous regressors (see Graham (2017), Yan et al. (2018), Jochmans and Otsu (2019), Dzemski (2019), Hughes (2026)). We build on the analysis of FernГЎndez-Val and Weidner (2016) because it allows for a broad class of panel and network models with both strictly exogenous and predetermined regressors. The problem of missing observations has received little attention in the large-рқ‘Ғ, рқ‘Ү literature on fixed effects estimators. FernГЎndez-Val and Weidner (2018) conjecture the asymptotic distributions and bias expressions of fixed effects estimators under a conditional independence assumption regarding the selection process. We relax this assumption to a conditional mean restriction, allowing for a more general selection process. We also show that one of their conjectured regularity assumptions is insufficient and must be strengthened to ensure the invertibility of the expected incidental parameter Hessian. Overall, we confirm their conjecture that bias-corrected estimators can be formed in a straightforward manner even when observations are missing. For one-way fixed effects estimators, Dhaene and Jochmans (2015) briefly show how the profile-likelihood correction can be adapted. Two further contributions on missing observations in large-рқ‘Ғ, рқ‘Ү settings are Su, Wang, and Wang (2026) and Czarnowske and Stammann (2026b). Both focus on linear models with interactive fixed effects, while we consider a broader class of models that includes nonlinear models with additive fixed effects. 

Outline . Section 2 introduces the model and the fixed effects estimators. Section 3 presents the asymptotic theory. Section 4 reports the simulation results. Section 5 concludes. 

Notation . Throughout the paper, P (В·) and E [В·] denote probability and expectation. A superscript вҖң0вҖқ on a parameter denotes its true population value. We write a. s. and wpa1 for almost surely and with probability approaching one, respectively. We use рқ‘ң рқ‘ғ (1) to denote a sequence of random variables that converges in probability to zero and Oрқ‘ғ (1) to denote a sequence that is bounded in probability. For two positive sequences рқ‘Һ рқ‘Ғрқ‘Ү and рқ‘Ҹ рқ‘Ғрқ‘Ү , we denote by рқ‘Һ рқ‘Ғрқ‘Ү вүҚ рқ‘Ҹ рқ‘Ғрқ‘Ү that the two sequences are of the same order of magnitude. We use рқ‘‘ 

вҲ’вҶ’ and рқ‘қ 

вҲ’вҶ’ to denote convergence in distribution and in probability, respectively. Unless otherwise stated, all stochastic statements are understood as almost-sure statements conditional on ОҰ, the sigma-algebra generated by the unobserved effects and initial conditions. 

# 2 Model and Estimator 

We observe panel data {( рқ‘Ұ рқ‘–рқ‘Ў , рқ‘Ҙ рқ‘–рқ‘Ў , рқ‘  рқ‘–рқ‘Ў ) : рқ‘– вҲҲ { 1, . . . , рқ‘Ғ }, рқ‘Ў вҲҲ { 1, . . . , рқ‘Ү }} , where рқ‘Ұ рқ‘–рқ‘Ў is an outcome variable, рқ‘Ҙ рқ‘–рқ‘Ў is a рқҗҫ -dimensional vector of predetermined regressors, and рқ‘  рқ‘–рқ‘Ў is a selection indicator that equals рқ‘  рқ‘–рқ‘Ў = 1 if рқ‘Ұ рқ‘–рқ‘Ў and every component of рқ‘Ҙ рқ‘–рқ‘Ў are observed, and рқ‘  рқ‘–рқ‘Ў = 0 otherwise. The panel is unbalanced whenever рқ‘  рқ‘–рқ‘Ў = 0 for some (рқ‘–, рқ‘Ў ).We factor the selection indicator as рқ‘  рқ‘–рқ‘Ў = рқ‘‘ рқ‘–рқ‘Ў рқ‘ҹ рқ‘–рқ‘Ў , where рқ‘‘ рқ‘–рқ‘Ў вҲҲ { 0, 1} is a ОҰ-measurable design indicator and рқ‘ҹ рқ‘–рқ‘Ў вҲҲ { 0, 1} is a response indicator. 2Definition 1 (Selection Process) . The selection process is deterministic if рқ‘ҹ рқ‘–рқ‘Ў = 1 for all рқ‘–, рқ‘Ў, рқ‘Ғ, рқ‘Ү ,

stochastic if рқ‘‘ рқ‘–рқ‘Ў = 1 for all рқ‘–, рқ‘Ў, рқ‘Ғ, рқ‘Ү , and mixed otherwise. Deterministic selection arises from the sampling design when, for example, entry and exit dates depend on unobserved effects. Stochastic selection arises from nonresponse or attrition, which may depend on past outcomes. The mixed case combines the two. Our simulation experiments in Section 4 study such a mixed design. рқ‘Ө )2 вүҘ рқ‘җ рқ‘Ҡ a. s. (vi) Design: There exist constants рқ‘җ рқ‘Ҳ , рқ‘җ рқ‘Ӯ вҲҲ (0, 1] such that inf рқ‘– рқ‘Ү вҲ’1 ГҚрқ‘Ү рқ‘Ў =1 рқ‘‘ рқ‘–рқ‘Ў вүҘ рқ‘җ рқ‘Ҳ and 

inf рқ‘Ў вү рқ‘Ў вҖІ рқ‘Ғ вҲ’1 ГҚрқ‘Ғ рқ‘– =1 рқ‘‘ рқ‘–рқ‘Ў рқ‘‘ рқ‘–рқ‘Ў вҖІ вүҘ рқ‘җ рқ‘Ӯ a. s. uniformly over рқ‘Ғ, рқ‘Ү .

Remark 1 (Assumption 1) . We comment on each part of Assumption 1 in turn. (i) The asymptotic framework, in which the cross-sectional and time dimensions diverge at proportional rates, is the same as in Hahn and Kuersteiner (2011) and FernГЎndez-Val and Weidner (2016). (ii) We restrict dependence in the sample {( рқ‘Ұ рқ‘–рқ‘Ў , рқ‘Ҙ рқ‘–рқ‘Ў , рқ‘ҹ рқ‘–рқ‘Ў )} in two ways: independence across units conditional on ОҰ, and рқӣј -mixing (strong mixing) within units, with coefficients that decay at a polynomial rate. Importantly, we do not require рқ‘ҹ рқ‘–рқ‘Ў to be independent of (рқ‘Ұ рқ‘–рқ‘Ў , рқ‘Ҙ рқ‘–рқ‘Ў )

conditional on ОҰ. That is, we do not assume missing-at-random, which this literature commonly invokes to treat the missingness as ignorable. We follow Hahn and Kuersteiner (2011) and FernГЎndez-Val and Weidner (2016) and use рқӣј -mixing because it is the weakest among the standard mixing conditions in econometrics and is preserved under measurable transformations. Any other form of weak dependence under which the regularity conditions underlying our results remain satisfied could replace it. (iii) We impose a conditional mean restriction to identify the model parameters. The regressors and the response indicator may be predetermined. Both may be arbitrarily related to past outcomes, but not to contemporaneous or future ones. Together with the affine score 

5(4) , the conditional mean restriction implies that the score is conditionally mean-zero, 

E (d1рқң“ )рқ‘–рқ‘Ў | C рқ‘Ў рқ‘– 

 = 0 for all рқ‘–, рқ‘Ў, рқ‘Ғ, рқ‘Ү . We use this repeatedly below. Beyond the first moment, the conditional distribution of рқ‘Ұ рқ‘–рқ‘Ў is left unrestricted. Imposing further restrictions on higher-order conditional moments, as in FernГЎndez-Val and Weidner (2016), would allow one to exploit Bartlett identities (see Bartlett 1953) to simplify the bias and variance expressions. This can improve the finite-sample performance of the debiased estimator, as noted by FernГЎndez-Val (2009). The normalization ensures unique identification. (iv) We adopt smoothness and moment conditions of the same form as in FernГЎndez-Val and Weidner (2016). The dominating function ОЁ(( рқ‘Ұ рқ‘–рқ‘Ў , рқ‘Ҙ вҖІ 

> рқ‘–рқ‘Ў

)) uniformly bounds both the regressors and all derivatives of the criterion function up to fourth order. Requiring 

sup рқ‘–рқ‘Ў E (ОЁ(( рқ‘Ұ рқ‘–рқ‘Ў , рқ‘Ҙ вҖІ 

> рқ‘–рқ‘Ў

))) рқӣҝ  to be a. s. uniformly bounded over рқ‘Ғ, рқ‘Ү ensures that these en-velopes have a finite рқӣҝ -th moment. Compared to FernГЎndez-Val and Weidner (2016), we allow рқӣҝ вүҘ 6 + рқңҲ instead of fixing рқӣҝ = 8 + рқңҲ . The choice of рқӣҝ affects, for example, the bandwidth of the debiased estimator defined below. (v) The convexity condition has two parts. The first part requires the response-weighted expected second derivative of the criterion function with respect to рқңӢ рқ‘–рқ‘Ў to be a. s. bounded away from zero, uniformly over the (рқ‘–, рқ‘Ў )-pairs with рқ‘‘ рқ‘–рқ‘Ў = 1 and over рқ‘Ғ, рқ‘Ү . This ensures local strict convexity in рқңҷ and is needed for the asymptotic expansions. Together with (iv), the first part implies that inf {( рқ‘–,рқ‘Ў ) : рқ‘‘ рқ‘–рқ‘Ў =1} E [рқ‘ҹ рқ‘–рқ‘Ў ] > 0 a. s. uniformly over рқ‘–, рқ‘Ў, рқ‘Ғ, рқ‘Ү , i.e., the (рқ‘–, рқ‘Ў )-pairs with рқ‘‘ рқ‘–рқ‘Ў = 1 have positive response probabilities. The second part is a generalized non-collinearity condition. Together, the two parts imply strict convexity of the expected objective function over the relevant part of the parameter space. (vi) We place two conditions on the ОҰ-measurable design component of the selection process. The first requires that, for each individual, the fraction of periods with рқ‘‘ рқ‘–рқ‘Ў = 1 be bounded away from zero, uniformly over рқ‘Ғ, рқ‘Ү . The second requires that any two periods рқ‘Ў вү  рқ‘Ў вҖІ

share a fraction of units with рқ‘‘ рқ‘–рқ‘Ў = рқ‘‘ рқ‘–рқ‘Ў вҖІ = 1 that is bounded away from zero, uniformly over 

рқ‘Ғ, рқ‘Ү . The second condition can be interpreted as an overlap, or connectivity, condition. Without it, the design could split the (рқ‘–, рқ‘Ў )-pairs into blocks of periods with no units in common, producing a block-specific identification problem between рқӣј and рқӣҫ . Because рқ‘‘ рқ‘–рқ‘Ў 

may itself depend on the unobserved effects or initial conditions, we impose both conditions almost surely. Since ГҚрқ‘Ғ рқ‘– =1 рқ‘‘ рқ‘–рқ‘Ў вүҘ ГҚрқ‘Ғ рқ‘– =1 рқ‘‘ рқ‘–рқ‘Ў рқ‘‘ рқ‘–рқ‘Ў вҖІ for any рқ‘Ў вү  рқ‘Ў вҖІ, the second condition implies 

inf рқ‘Ў рқ‘Ғ вҲ’1 ГҚрқ‘Ғ рқ‘– =1 рқ‘‘ рқ‘–рқ‘Ў вүҘ рқ‘җ рқ‘Ӯ for рқ‘Ү вүҘ 2.

## 3.3 Bias Correction 

The asymptotic distribution of ЛҶрқӣҪ is not centered at zero, which invalidates standard inference. We therefore propose an analytical bias correction. It adjusts ЛҶрқӣҪ using estimates of the two bias terms, which are constructed as sample analogs of the expressions in Theorem 1, with the true parameter values replaced by the corresponding fixed effects estimates. We also briefly discuss alternative inference procedures based on the split-panel jackknife of FernГЎndez-Val and Weidner (2016) and the jackknife рқ‘Ў рқ‘һ -statistic of Higgins (2026). 8Let (ddrрқң“ )рқ‘–рқ‘Ў and ( ddr

Remark 3 (Theorem 2) . Again, our results nest those of FernГЎndez-Val and Weidner (2016) for balanced panels. The analytical bias correction does not inflate the variance, i.e., the uncorrected and the debiased estimator have the same asymptotic variance. The correction factor рқң” рқ‘– (рқ‘ҡ ) is adapted to allow for gaps in individual time series. For balanced panels it collapses to the factor proposed in FernГЎndez-Val and Weidner (2016), рқң” рқ‘– (рқ‘ҡ ) = рқ‘Ү /( рқ‘Ү вҲ’ рқ‘ҡ ) for all рқ‘–, рқ‘Ғ . Consistency of 

9bрқҗө рқӣј requires рқңҚ вҲҲ ( 0, (рқң… вҲ’ 1)/( 2рқң… )) . Within this range the choice of bandwidth is asymptotically immaterial. The rate-optimal exponent рқңҚ вҲ— = (рқң… вҲ’ 1)/( 6рқң…рқӣҝ ) nonetheless lies near the lower end, favoring small exponents and hence rather small bandwidths relative to рқ‘Ү . This is in line with FernГЎndez-Val and Weidner (2016), who recommend bandwidths no larger than four and who also recommend reporting results for several bandwidths. Increasing рқң… widens the admissible range of exponents, with its upper limit approaching 1/2, yet drives рқңҚ вҲ— toward zero (recall 

рқӣҝ = 2рқң… + рқңҲ ). Finally, the researcher need not know whether the regressors or the selection process are predetermined. The proposed bias correction is valid in either case. 

Remark 4 (Jackknife Inference) . Alternatively, one may conduct inference using the split-panel jackknife of FernГЎndez-Val and Weidner (2016) or the jackknife рқ‘Ў рқ‘һ -statistic of Higgins (2026), at the cost of an additional unconditional homogeneity assumption: {( рқ‘Ұ рқ‘–рқ‘Ў , рқ‘Ҙ вҖІ 

> рқ‘–рқ‘Ў

, рқ‘  рқ‘–рқ‘Ў , рқӣј рқ‘– , рқӣҫ рқ‘Ў ) : рқ‘– вҲҲ{1, . . . , рқ‘Ғ }, рқ‘Ў вҲҲ { 1, . . . , рқ‘Ү }} is identically distributed across рқ‘– and strictly stationary across рқ‘Ў for all рқ‘Ғ, рқ‘Ү . Unlike the rest of the paper, this assumption is not conditional on ОҰ. A design рқ‘‘ рқ‘–рқ‘Ў that is non-random given ОҰ is unconditionally random whenever it depends on unobserved effects or initial conditions. Thus, рқ‘  рқ‘–рқ‘Ў is also unconditionally random. The assumption is sufficient to ensure that the biases are homogeneous across all subsamples. It rules out, for example, structural breaks or time trends in the data-generating processes of the observed variables (including the selection process) and the unobserved effects. Section 4 examines the consequences of violating this assumption. The resampling strategies proposed in FernГЎndez-Val and Weidner (2016) and Higgins (2026) carry over unchanged to unbalanced panels. 

# 4 Simulation Experiments 

We study the finite-sample behavior of the uncorrected and debiased fixed effects estimators for the parameters of a dynamic probit model in unbalanced panels. We adapt the data-generating process of FernГЎndez-Val and Weidner (2016) for dynamic probit models to unbalanced panels. For debiasing, we consider analytical bias corrections with в„Һ вҲҲ { 0, 1, 2} and jackknife corrections, forming subsamples in the same way as for balanced panels. We report the bias relative to the true parameter value (in percent), the coverage rate of confidence intervals with a 95% nominal level, and the average length of these intervals. The first 101 periods, indexed by рқ‘Ў вҲҲ {вҲ’ 100 , . . . , 0}, serve as a burn-in for рқӣҫ рқ‘Ў , рқ‘Ұ рқ‘–рқ‘Ў , рқ‘Ҙ рқ‘–рқ‘Ў , and рқ‘ҹ рқ‘–рқ‘Ў .Estimation uses рқ‘Ў вҲҲ { 1, . . . , рқ‘Ү } only. We initialize the processes at рқ‘Ў = вҲ’100 by drawing рқ‘Ұ рқ‘– (вҲ’ 100 )

and рқ‘ҹ рқ‘– (вҲ’ 100 ) from a Bernoulli distribution with a success probability of 1/2 each, and рқ‘Ҙ рқ‘– (вҲ’ 100 )

from a standard normal distribution. For рқ‘– вҲҲ { 1, . . . , рқ‘Ғ } and рқ‘Ў вҲҲ {вҲ’ 99 , . . . , рқ‘Ү },

The selection process is mixed рқ‘  рқ‘–рқ‘Ў = рқ‘‘ рқ‘–рқ‘Ў рқ‘ҹ рқ‘–рқ‘Ў . The design is a deterministic function of рқӣј рқ‘– and is therefore ОҰ-measurable, whereas the response component is stochastic and predetermined. We set рқӣҪ рқ‘Ұ = 0.5 and рқӣҪ рқ‘Ҙ = 1, and generate samples with рқ‘Ғ = 200 and рқ‘Ү вҲҲ { 10 , 20 , 40 }. The intercept рқңҢ 0 of the response equation is calibrated so that, on average, half of the observations are missing: рқңҢ 0 = 0.095 for рқ‘Ү = 10 , рқңҢ 0 = 0.260 for рқ‘Ү = 20 , and рқңҢ 0 = 0.350 for рқ‘Ү = 40 . All results are based on 10 ,000 simulated samples for each configuration. The design component violates the unconditional homogeneity assumption underlying the jackknife corrections (see Remark 4). Because рқӣј рқ‘– is i.i.d. across рқ‘– , and рқ‘’ рқ‘– and рқ‘“ рқ‘– depend on рқ‘– only through рқӣј рқ‘– , the vector (рқ‘Ұ рқ‘–рқ‘Ў , рқ‘Ҙ вҖІ 

> рқ‘–рқ‘Ў

, рқ‘  рқ‘–рқ‘Ў , рқӣј рқ‘– , рқӣҫ рқ‘Ў ) remains identically distributed across рқ‘– for all рқ‘Ғ . It is not, however, strictly stationary across рқ‘Ў . Since рқ‘“ рқ‘– < рқ‘Ү for every unit with 4|рқӣј рқ‘– | вүӨ 1, P (рқ‘‘ рқ‘–рқ‘Ў = 1)

falls by рқ‘Ў = рқ‘Ү to the share of units present throughout, 2 {1 вҲ’ рқҗ№ N (1)} вүҲ 0.32 . Because рқ‘  рқ‘–рқ‘Ў = рқ‘‘ рқ‘–рқ‘Ў рқ‘ҹ рқ‘–рқ‘Ў ,the selection probability P (рқ‘  рқ‘–рқ‘Ў = 1) likewise peaks around рқ‘Ү half and declines thereafter to a value strictly below 0.32 . Consequently, the subsamples formed across рқ‘– , which correct рқҗө рқӣҫ, вҲһ, remain homogeneous, whereas the subsamples formed across рқ‘Ў , which correct рқҗө рқӣј, вҲһ, do not. Table 1 reports the results. The uncorrected estimator is severely biased, particularly for рқӣҪ рқ‘Ұ .The bias in рқӣҪ рқ‘Ҙ is smaller but still distorts coverage. The analytical correction with a positive bandwidth removes most of the bias and restores coverage close to the nominal level. The correction with в„Һ = 0 ignores the feedback bias, which predominates for ЛҶрқӣҪ рқ‘Ұ . Because the regressor рқ‘Ҙ рқ‘–рқ‘Ў is strictly exogenous, в„Һ = 0 removes most of the bias in ЛҶрқӣҪ рқ‘Ҙ . Nonetheless, positive bandwidths perform better, reflecting the feedback bias arising from both the lagged outcome and the predetermined selection process. Performance is similar across positive bandwidths once рқ‘Ү 

is moderately large. For рқ‘Ү = 10 , the smaller bandwidth ( в„Һ = 1) clearly dominates. Both patterns are consistent with Remark 3, which favors small bandwidths. The two jackknife corrections share the same point estimate and differ only in their standard errors. They overcorrect the bias in ЛҶрқӣҪ рқ‘Ұ because the design renders the subsamples formed across рқ‘Ў heterogeneous. The split-panel jackknife of FernГЎndez-Val and Weidner (2016) often undercovers, while the рқ‘Ў 2-statistic of Higgins (2026) is more robust, achieving nominal coverage at the cost of substantially wider intervals. 12 5 Concluding Remarks 

We have studied fixed effects M-estimation for unbalanced panels under selection that is ОҰ-measurable in its design component and stochastic in its response component. We do not assume missing-at-random as common in the literature. The response may depend on past outcomes, regressors, and unobserved effects. Feedback bias can arise from a predetermined selection process, even when the regressors are strictly exogenous. Moreover, selection that depends on unobserved effects can render subsamples heterogeneous, thereby invalidating jackknife corrections. Our analytical correction does not rely on such homogeneity. It is valid whether or not the regressors and the selection process are predetermined, and the researcher need not know which case applies. For applied work, we recommend a positive but small bandwidth and suggest reporting results for several bandwidths. Extending the analysis to estimators with non-smooth criterion functions, such as quantile regression, is a natural direction for future work. 13 References

15 Appendix 

# A Notation and Definitions 

Before presenting the proofs, we provide additional definitions and comment on the notation used throughout the Appendix and Online Appendix. 

Unobserved Effects Models. The linear index is рқңӢ рқ‘–рқ‘Ў (рқӣҪ, рқңҮ рқ‘–рқ‘Ў (рқңҷ )) = (рқ‘Ӣ рқӣҪ )рқ‘–рқ‘Ў + ( рқҗ·рқңҷ )рқ‘–рқ‘Ў , with рқ‘Ӣ a dense 

рқ‘Ғрқ‘Ү Г— рқҗҫ matrix and рқҗ· a sparse рқ‘Ғрқ‘Ү Г— рқҗҝ matrix of binary indicators. We write рқңӢ (рқӣҪ, рқңҷ ) := рқ‘Ӣ рқӣҪ + рқҗ·рқңҷ .

# S.1 Proof of Asymptotic Expansions 

Partial Derivatives of Objective Function. Given the criterion function рқң“ (рқ‘Ұ, рқңӢ ) and the linear index рқңӢ (рқӣҪ, рқңҷ ) = рқ‘Ӣ рқӣҪ + рқҗ·рқңҷ , the first- and second-order derivatives of Lрқ‘Ғрқ‘Ү (рқӣҪ, рқңҷ ) involving the constraint term рқ‘Јрқ‘Ј вҖІ are 

рқң• Lрқ‘Ғрқ‘Ү (рқӣҪ, рқңҷ )

рқң• рқӣҪ = рқ‘Ӣ вҖІSd1рқң“ (рқӣҪ, рқңҷ )вҲҡрқ‘Ғрқ‘Ү , рқң• Lрқ‘Ғрқ‘Ү (рқӣҪ, рқңҷ )

рқң•рқңҷ = рқ‘ў (рқӣҪ, рқңҷ ) + рқ‘Јрқ‘Ј вҖІрқңҷ 

вҲҡрқ‘Ғрқ‘Ү ,рқң• 2Lрқ‘Ғрқ‘Ү (рқӣҪ, рқңҷ )

рқң•рқңҷрқң•рқңҷ вҖІ = рқҗ» (рқӣҪ, рқңҷ ) .

All remaining derivatives take the unified form 

рқң• r+2Lрқ‘Ғрқ‘Ү (рқӣҪ, рқңҷ )

рқң•рқңғ 1 В· В· В· рқң•рқңғ r рқң• рқңҡ рқң• рқңҡ вҖІ = рқҗҙ вҖІ вҲҮr+2рқң“ (рқӣҪ, рқңҷ ) diag (рқ‘Ө 1 вҠҷ В· В· В· вҠҷ рқ‘Ө r) рқҗө 

вҲҡрқ‘Ғрқ‘Ү ,

where r вүҘ 0, рқңҡ вҲҲ { рқӣҪ, рқңҷ } with рқҗҙ = рқ‘Ӣ if рқңҡ = рқӣҪ and рқҗҙ = рқҗ· if рқңҡ = рқңҷ (and similarly рқҗө for рқңҡ вҖІ); each scalar differentiator рқңғ рқ‘Һ вҲҲ { рқӣҪ рқ‘ҳ , рқңҷ рқ‘ҷ } contributes рқ‘Ө рқ‘Һ = рқ‘Ӣрқ‘’ рқ‘ҳ or рқ‘Ө рқ‘Һ = рқҗ·рқ‘’ рқ‘ҷ ; and for r = 0 the diag factor is absent. This covers the рқң• 2Lрқ‘ӣ /рқң• рқӣҪрқң• рқӣҪ вҖІ and рқң• 2Lрқ‘ӣ /рқң• рқӣҪрқң•рқңҷ вҖІ blocks ( r = 0) as well as all third- and fourth-order mixed derivatives.

The stated result follows from Theorem 4, i.e., вҲҡрқ‘Ғрқ‘Ү вҲҘ ЛҶрқӣҪ вҲ’ рқӣҪ 0 вҲҘ2 = Oрқ‘ғ (1). в–Ў

Corollary S.1 (Consistency of ЛҶрқңӢ ,) . Let Lemma 2 hold. Then, for 1 вүӨ рқ‘қ вүӨ 2рқң… , вҲҘ ЛҶрқңӢ вҲ’ рқңӢ 0 вҲҘ рқ‘қ =

Oрқ‘ғ (( рқ‘Ғрқ‘Ү )вҲ’1/4+1/рқ‘қ ), and вҲҘ ЛҶрқңӢ вҲ’ рқңӢ 0 вҲҘвҲһ = Oрқ‘ғ (( рқ‘Ғрқ‘Ү )вҲ’1/4+1/( 4рқң… ) ).

Proof of Corollary S.1. Recall рқңӢ (рқӣҪ, рқңҷ ) = рқ‘Ӣ рқӣҪ + рқҗ·рқңҷ . The stated results follow by HГ¶lderвҖҷs inequality, JensenвҖҷs inequality, the triangle inequality, Lemma 2, and Theorem 4. в–Ў

## S.2.1 Proof of Lemma 2 

# (i) By the triangle inequality and вҲҘ рқҗҙ вҠ— рқҗө вҲҘ = вҲҘ рқҗҙ вҲҘвҲҘ рқҗө вҲҘ, вҲҘрқҗ· вҲҘ1 = O( вҲҡрқ‘Ғрқ‘Ү ). Analogously,

## S.2.2 Proof of Lemma 3 

# (i) By a Taylor expansion of bрқ‘Җ рқ‘Ӣрқ‘’ рқ‘ҳ = рқ‘Җ ( ЛҶрқӣҪ, ЛҶрқңҷ ) рқ‘Ӣрқ‘’ рқ‘ҳ around (рқӣҪ 0, рқңҷ 0) for each рқ‘ҳ вҲҲ { 1, . . . , рқҗҫ }

(see Feng et al. (2014)), bрқ‘Җ рқ‘Ӣрқ‘’ рқ‘ҳ вҲ’ рқ‘Җ рқ‘Ӣрқ‘’ рқ‘ҳ = вҲ’ qрқ‘„ }вҲҮ3рқң“ diag ( qрқ‘Җ рқ‘Ӣрқ‘’ рқ‘ҳ )( ЛҶрқңӢ вҲ’ рқңӢ 0), where ЛҮрқӣҪ and ЛҮрқңҷ may differ for each element and each рқ‘ҳ . By HГ¶lderвҖҷs inequality, Lemma 2, and Corollary S.1, 

max рқ‘ҳ вҲҘ bрқ‘Җ рқ‘Ӣрқ‘’ рқ‘ҳ вҲ’ рқ‘Җ рқ‘Ӣрқ‘’ рқ‘ҳ вҲҘ2 вүӨ вҲҘ qрқ‘„ вҲҘ2 вҲҘ }d3рқң“ вҲҘ6 max рқ‘ҳ вҲҘ qрқ‘Җ рқ‘Ӣрқ‘’ рқ‘ҳ вҲҘ6 вҲҘ ЛҶрқңӢ вҲ’ рқңӢ 0 вҲҘ6 = Oрқ‘ғ (( рқ‘Ғрқ‘Ү )1/4). Hence, 

max рқ‘ҳ вҲҘ bрқ‘Җ рқ‘Ӣрқ‘’ рқ‘ҳ вҲ’ рқ‘Җ рқ‘Ӣрқ‘’ рқ‘ҳ вҲҘ рқ‘қ = Oрқ‘ғ (( рқ‘Ғрқ‘Ү )вҲ’1/4+1/рқ‘қ ) for 1 вүӨ рқ‘қ вүӨ 2.# (ii) For r вҲҲ { 1, 2, 3}, by a Taylor expansion of ddrрқң“ = drрқң“ ( ЛҶрқӣҪ, ЛҶрқңҷ ) around (рқӣҪ 0, рқңҷ 0) (see Feng et al. (2014)), ddrрқң“ вҲ’ drрқң“ = ~dr+1рқң“ ( ЛҶрқңӢ вҲ’ рқңӢ 0), where ЛҮрқӣҪ and ЛҮрқңҷ may differ for each element. By HГ¶lderвҖҷs inequality, Lemma 2, and Corollary S.1, вҲҘddrрқң“ вҲ’ drрқң“ вҲҘ2рқң… вүӨ вҲҘ ~dr+1рқң“ вҲҘ2рқң… вҲҘ ЛҶрқңӢ вҲ’ рқңӢ 0 вҲҘвҲһ =

Oрқ‘ғ (( рқ‘Ғрқ‘Ү )вҲ’1/4+3/( 4рқң… ) ). Hence, вҲҘddrрқң“ вҲ’ drрқң“ вҲҘ рқ‘қ = Oрқ‘ғ (( рқ‘Ғрқ‘Ү )вҲ’1/4+1/( 4рқң… )+ 1/рқ‘қ ).# (iii) Since eрқҗ№ is diagonal and вҲҘрқҗ· вҖІ Вҹ(рқ‘  вҠҷ d2рқң“ )вҲҘ 2рқң… = Oрқ‘ғ (( рқ‘Ғрқ‘Ү )1/4+1/( 4рқң… ) ) by Lemma 2, вҲҘ eрқҗ№ вҲҘвҲһ =

вҲҘрқҗ· вҖІ Вҹ(рқ‘  вҠҷ d2рқң“ )вҲҘ вҲһ/вҲҡрқ‘Ғрқ‘Ү вүӨ вҲҘ рқҗ· вҖІ Вҹ(рқ‘  вҠҷ d2рқң“ )вҲҘ 2рқң… /вҲҡрқ‘Ғрқ‘Ү = Oрқ‘ғ (рқ‘Ү вҲ’1/4+1/( 4рқң… ) ).# (iv) By a Taylor expansion of рқҗ№ (рқӣҪ, рқңҷ ) around (рқӣҪ 0, рқңҷ 0), Lemmas 2 and S.3, the triangle inequality, and sup рқӣҪ вҲҘ рқӣҪ вҲ’ рқӣҪ 0 вҲҘ2 вүӨ рқңҖ and sup рқңҷ вҲҘрқңҷ вҲ’ рқңҷ 0 вҲҘрқ‘һ вүӨ рқңӮ , sup (рқӣҪ,рқңҷ ) вҲҘрқҗ№ (рқӣҪ, рқңҷ ) вҲ’ рқҗ№ вҲҘ рқ‘қ = рқ‘ң рқ‘ғ (1).By Lemma 2, the conditions of Lemma S.7 with ВҜрқ‘қ = вҲһ hold wpa1. Thus, рқҗ№ (рқӣҪ, рқңҷ ) is invertible for all (рқӣҪ, рқңҷ ) вҲҲ рқ”… (рқңҖ ) Г— рқ”“ (рқңӮ, рқ‘һ ) wpa1, and sup (рқӣҪ,рқңҷ ) вҲҘ( рқҗ№ (рқӣҪ, рқңҷ )) вҲ’1 вҲҘвҲһ = Oрқ‘ғ (1).# (v) By Theorem 4, ЛҶрқӣҪ and ЛҶрқңҷ are interior to рқ”… (рқңҖ ) Г— рқ”“ (рқңӮ, рқ‘һ ). Lemma S.7 (Invertibility) . Let 2 вүӨ рқ‘һ вүӨ рқ‘қ вүӨ ВҜрқ‘қ and let рқҗҙ (рқӣҪ, рқңҷ ) be symmetric on рқ”… (рқңҖ ) Г— рқ”“ (рқңӮ, рқ‘һ ).If рқҗҙ is invertible with вҲҘ рқҗҙ вҲ’1 вҲҘ ВҜрқ‘қ = O( 1), вҲҘ eрқҗҙ вҲҘ ВҜрқ‘қ = рқ‘ң рқ‘ғ (1), and sup вҲҘ рқҗҙ (рқӣҪ, рқңҷ ) вҲ’ рқҗҙ вҲҘ ВҜрқ‘қ = рқ‘ң рқ‘ғ (1), then 

рқҗҙ (рқӣҪ, рқңҷ ) > 0 wpa1 and sup вҲҘ( рқҗҙ (рқӣҪ, рқңҷ )) вҲ’1 вҲҘ рқ‘қ = Oрқ‘ғ (1) for 2 вүӨ рқ‘қ вүӨ ВҜрқ‘қ .

Proof of Lemma S.7. See Proof of Lemma 11 in Czarnowske and Stammann (2026a). в–Ў

Lemma S.8 (Neumann Series) . For symmetric рқҗҙ = рқҗҙ + eрқҗҙ with рқҗҙ invertible, вҲҘ рқҗҙ вҲ’1 вҲҘ рқ‘қ = O( 1),and вҲҘ eрқҗҙ вҲҘ рқ‘қ = рқ‘ң рқ‘ғ (1) for some рқ‘қ вүҘ 2, рқҗҙ вҲ’1 = рқҗҙ вҲ’1 ГҚрқ‘Ҹ вүҘ0 (вҲ’ eрқҗҙ рқҗҙ вҲ’1)рқ‘Ҹ wpa1. 

Proof of Lemma S.8. See Proof of Lemma 12 in Czarnowske and Stammann (2026a). в–Ў

Lemma S.9 (Approximation of Inverse) . For symmetric invertible рқҗҙ = рқҗө + рқҗё with рқҗө invertible: (i) рқҗҙ вҲ’1 = рқҗө вҲ’1 вҲ’ рқҗҙ вҲ’1рқҗё рқҗө вҲ’1; (ii) рқҗҙ вҲ’1 = рқҗө вҲ’1 вҲ’ рқҗө вҲ’1рқҗё рқҗө вҲ’1 + рқҗө вҲ’1рқҗё рқҗҙ вҲ’1рқҗё рқҗө вҲ’1.

Proof of Lemma S.9. See Proof of Lemma 13 in Czarnowske and Stammann (2026a). в–Ў
